    Development of Robust 17(R),18(S)-Epoxyeicosatetraenoic Acid (17,18-EEQ) Analogues as Potential Clinical Antiarrhythmic Agents

    Abstract

    17(R),18(S)-Epoxyeicosatetraenoic acid (EEQ) is a cytochrome P450 metabolite of eicosapentaenoic acid (EPA) and a powerful negative chronotrope with low nanomolar activity in a neonatal rat cardiomyocyte (NRCM) arrhythmia model. Prior studies identified oxamide 2b as a soluble epoxide hydrolase (sEH) stable replacement but unsuitable for in vivo applications due to limited oral bioavailability and metabolic stability. These ADME limitations have been addressed in an improved generation of negative chronotropes, e.g., 4 and 16, which were evaluated as potential clinical candidates.

    Introduction

    ARTICLE SECTIONS
    Jump To
    Cardiac arrhythmias often progress into serious cardiovascular complications(1) such as cardiac arrest or stroke and, thus, are important contributors to morbidity and mortality worldwide.(2) Current treatment options(3) include potentially risky and invasive procedures, e.g., electric shock therapy (cardioversion) and catheter ablation, and usually long-term medication.(4) Unfortunately, extant antiarrhythmic drugs, when used prophylactically or therapeutically, are often poorly tolerated because of their side effects(5) and have a disconcerting propensity toward inducing future arrhythmias.(6) Due to the various etiologies of arrhythmias, the development of safer and more effective antiarrhythmic drugs is very challenging and requires the discovery of pleiotropic agents effective at various stages of an arrhythmic episode.
    In recent decades, a large body of evidence has been garnered from epidemiological, animal, and clinical studies that purport polyunsaturated Ï-3 fatty acids (Ï-3 PUFAs), primarily the eicosapentaenoic (EPA) and docosahexaenoic (DHA) acids commonly found in fish oils, have rhythm stabilizing effects and are beneficial for the prevention and/or amelioration of arrhythmias arising from diverse origins.(7â9) Specifically, EPA prevents and/or terminates arrhythmias caused by Î²-adrenergic agonists and calcium overload,(10) which suggests EPA offers multimodal benefits in prophylactic and therapeutic applications for the management of arrhythmias.(11) It is also reassuring that the above referenced clinical studies concluded Ï-3 PUFA supplementation is well tolerated, safe, and synergistic with existing therapies. On the other hand, some clinical studies of dietary fish oil or EPA ester dietary supplementation have proven inconclusive(12,13) or proposed narrowly defined utility.(14)
    Pioneering studies(15) by Schunck and colleagues into the mechanisms of action and molecular species responsible for the antiarrhythmic and cardioprotective effects of EPA and its congeners implicated the cytochrome P450 (CYP) branch of the eicosanoid cascade as an important participant.(16) Noteworthy observations include the following: (i) dietary EPA/DHA ester supplementation reduced arrhythmia as well as sudden cardiac death in an angiotensin (Ang) II-induced hypertension study using rats;(17) (ii) EPA is equal or preferred over arachidonic acid (AA) as a substrate by most CYP epoxygenases;(18) (iii) CYP-epoxyeicosanoid reservoirs are present at the sn-2 position of phospholipids and readily released by phospholipase (PL) A2;(19) (iv) the principal CYP epoxygenase isoforms preferentially metabolize EPA to the 17,18-epoxide (17,18-EEQ), including most relevantly the major cardiac isoform, CYP2J;(18,20â22) (v) transgenic overexpression of human CYP2J2 protects against arrhythmia susceptibility in mouse models of cardiac hypertrophy;(23) (vi) 17,18-EEQ is the main fatty acid epoxide in the heart and other organs of rats fed an Ï-3 PUFA rich diet but not when ingesting an Ï-6 PUFA diet;(18) (vii) EPA epoxides and/or their hydrolysis products have been detected in humans in vivo, and their levels were found to increase in the plasma and urine of human volunteers ingesting daily multigram amounts of Ï-3 PUFA esters;(24â26) and (viii) EPA and 17,18-EEQ are non-angiogenic unlike some AA metabolites.(27)
    The effects of EPA (3â10 Î¼M) in a neonatal rat cardiomyocyte (NRCM) arrhythmia model,(11) e.g., restoration to basal beating rate and termination of Ca2+-induced fibrillation, could be reproduced by 17,18-EEQ but at a 1000-fold lower concentration and with almost an immediate effect as compared with the 10â30 min delay needed for EPA (Figure 1).(18,28) Reflective of the dominant stereoselectivity in the biosynthesis of 17,18-EEQ (vide supra), the R,S-enantiomer dose-dependently reduces the spontaneous beating rate of neonatal cardiomyocytes with an EC50 of 1â2 nM, whereas its antipode has the opposite, albeit far less potent, effect.(28)
    In common with other CYP PUFA autacoids,(29,30) 17,18-EEQ is chemically and metabolically labile which constrains its study in vivo as well as its therapeutic utility. Prominent degradative/catabolic processes for epoxyeicosanoids include (i) autoxidation, (ii) Î²-oxidation, (iii) esterification, and (iv) metabolism by other pathways of the eicosanoid cascade, viz., cytochromes P450 (CYPs), cyclooxygenases (COXs), lipoxygenases (LOXs), and soluble epoxide hydrolase (sEH).(31) The latter converts 17,18-EEQ to the corresponding vic-17,18-diol and nullifies the ability to regulate NRCM beating rates.(18)
    A systematic program to elucidate the pharmacophore responsible for the chronotropic activity of 17,18-EEQ identified the carboxylic acid, cis-Î11,12-olefin, and 17(R),18(S)-epoxide as key structural elements.(28) Consequently, 17,18-EEQ could be simplified to 1 which was more accessible synthetically and precluded autoxidation as well as metabolism by COXs and LOXs (Figure 1). The second generation of analogues, illustrated by 2, imparted more drug-like properties and witnessed the introduction of epoxide bioisosteres including ureas and oxamides. These epoxide mimetics were conveniently often solids, dispensed with the two stereocenters without loss of efficacy, modestly improved hydrophilicity, and, most importantly, obviated sEH inactivation. In the present study, we address the remaining Î²-oxidation and esterification liabilities in preparation for pharmacokinetic (PK) studies, in vivo evaluation in animal arrhythmia models, and to expedite progress toward the clinical introduction of a first-in-class therapeutic.

    Results and Discussion

    ARTICLE SECTIONS
    Jump To
    The third generation analogues shown in Table 1 were screened initially for their chronotropic effects in our NRCM assay and for inhibition of recombinant sEH at 1 and 10 Î¼M (Table 1). Since replacement of the Î²-carbon of PUFAs with oxygen abolishes(32,33) Î²-oxidation and profoundly limits esterification into phospholipids and glycerides,(34) the 3-oxa versions of 2a and b, i.e., 3 and 4, respectively, were prepared. In addition to improving water solubility by 2â3 orders of magnitude (vide infra), this simple atomic substitution boosted the negative chronotropic impact of urea 3 by ca. 40%, whereas that of oxamide 4 remained essentially unchanged. As expected based upon earlier studies,(30)4 was also the weaker sEH inhibitor of the two analogues. The aqueous solubilities of triethylene glycol (TEG) esters 5 and 6 as well as glycine amide 7 improved compared with their parent carboxylates, but later studies revealed they were quickly hydrolyzed in biologic milieu. The more hindered N-methylglycine amide 8 and alanine amide 9 proved less proficient than 7 at suppressing the beating rate of the NRCMs. This can be attributed to the reduced rate of amidase hydrolysis of 8 and 9 to the free carboxylic acid which is necessary for biological activity. With its ionizable proton that mimics the carboxylate in 1 and 2 at physiologic pH, N-phenylsulphonimide 10 restored the activity to an acceptable level, as did sulfonate 11 and phosphonate 13. Since the latter is dibasic, it was of interest to probe the influence of charge on biologic activity.(35) We anticipated phosphodiesterases would rapidly cleave 12 into dianion 13, whereas the mixed methyl/pivaloyloxymethyldiester analogue 14 would in the short term furnish a monoanion, and the dipivaloyloxymethyldiester 15 might survive intact long enough to penetrate the cellular membrane and undergo intracellular cleavage. In the event, there was no clear advantage from any of the three compared with disodium salt 13. Of several traditional carboxylate bioisosteres,(36) tetrazole 16 was the most potent in the NRCM assay followed by oxathiadiazole 17, while the structurally related oxadiazol-5-one 18 proved disappointing, likely due to the much reduced acidity of oxadiazol-5-ones compared with tetrazoles and oxathiadiazoles. Despite the utilization of an oxamide epoxide bioisostere, 19 was second only to urea 6 for inhibition of sEH; this would appear to be an unrecognized property of the benzothiazolylacetamide moiety. The poor performance of phenol 20 is consistent with the earlier SAR study(28) that identified a carboxylic acid or comparably acidic functional group as essential for full biological activity of CYP epoxyeicosanoids. Departing from the linear aliphatic template utilized thus far, analogues 21 and 22 demonstrate the Î11,12-olefin can be replaced with an arene without loss of efficacy, thus suggesting wider options for future development.

    Oxamides 4 and 16 were selected for the next tier of evaluations based upon potency and their minimal sEH inhibition which in toto more closely represent the pharmacological profile of 17,18-EEQ. The potential advantages and disadvantages of sEH inhibitors versus epoxyeicosanoid mimetics as therapeutics have been thoroughly discussed recently.(16) Chief among the many strategic considerations of sEH inhibition by the analogues was the unpredictable consequences of the accumulation of endogenous epoxy-PUFAs [e.g., leukotoxins (EpOMEs) and epoxyeicosatrienoic acids (EETs)] which can vary depending among other things on tissue, disease state, and nutritional intake of PUFAs.
    Inspection of the dose response curves for 17,18-EEQ, 2b, 4, and 16 (Figure 2) reveals a progressive generational improvement in potency in the order of 17,18-EEQ < 2b < 4 â 16. The Emax for negative chronotropic activity, on the other hand, was about the same for all of the synthetic analogues (Figure 2).
    2b, 4, and 16 displayed similarly high human plasma stability and percent plasma protein binding (PPB), but the in vitro clearance rate of 2b using human hepatocytes was â¼6.6 and â¼3.5 times faster than 4 and 16, respectively (Table 2). However, it was the latterâs significant inhibition of CYP2C9, CYP2C19, and CYP3A4 that relegated this analogue to a lower priority.

    In contrast to 2b and 4, analogue 16 also inhibited the biosynthesis of EEQs from EPA by CYP2J2, the major CYP epoxygenase in human heart (Figure 3). Analogue 16 shared this property with the currently used antiarrhythmic drugs amiodarone and dronedarone as well as with the antihistamine terfenadine (Figure 3). As discussed recently, drug-mediated inhibition of CYP2J2-catalyzed formation of endogenous cardioprotective epoxyeicosanoids might induce adverse cardiac effects.(37,38)
    Studies with human liver microsomes indicate that analogue 16 is not only a potent inhibitor but also an effective substrate for CYP enzymes. As shown in Figure 4, 16 was rapidly metabolized to [+O] and [+O + H2O] metabolites. The reaction sequence presumably consisted of CYP-catalyzed epoxidation of the double bond, followed by hydration to a diol through the action of microsomal and/or soluble epoxide hydrolases, which are present in human liver microsomes.(39) In contrast to 16, analogues 2b and 4 remained comparatively stable (Figure 4). After 60 min of incubation, about 40% of 16, 3.9% of 2b, and 1.5% of 4 were converted to the corresponding oxygenated metabolites (sum of [+O] and [+O + H2O] metabolites), as calculated from the relative peak areas in LC-MS analysis.
    The influences of the 3-oxa and tetrazole bioisostere on PK are evident in the 24 h plasma concentrationâtime curves of 4 and 16 vs 2b in rat (Figure 5). Maximum plasma levels were reached about 1.6 (2b and 4) and 2 h (16) after oral administration, indicating rapid absorption of all three analogues. However, the maximum plasma levels for 4 and 16 were approximately 2 orders of magnitude higher and remained elevated far longer than for 2b (Figure 5, panel A). Oral bioavailabilities were almost 100% with 4 and 35% with 16, as calculated from the dose-corrected areas under the curve after oral (Figure 5, panel A) versus intravenous (Figure 5, panel B) administration. Factors contributing to the higher oral bioavailability of 4 compared to 16 are so far unknown but may include differences in their physicochemical properties (for pKa and logD values; see Table 2) as well as gastrointestinal absorption sites. In the elimination phase, 4 showed a considerably higher half-life than 16 (9.6 vs 3.6 h after oral administration), in line with the different in vitro stabilities of these analogues in liver microsomes (Figure 4) and hepatocytes (Table 2).
    Based upon the foregoing results, 4 was selected for allometric scaling in three mammalian species using a linear regression model with the log of animal mass as the X variable and log of half-life as the Y variable (Figure 6). Based on this allometric scaling, a t1/2 of 37.9 h is predicted in humans with a standard weight of 75 kg (Y = 0.2742*X + 1.064).
    To obtain a first in vivo proof of concept regarding the antiarrhythmic properties of synthetic 17,18-EEQ analogues, compound 5 was tested in a rat model of acute myocardial infarction. In this model, coronary artery ligation is used to induce the development of spontaneous ventricular tachycardia. Continuous ECG recording showed that pretreating the animals with compound 5 significantly reduced the total time in arrhythmia as well as the severity of the arrhythmic episodes compared with the vehicle control (Figure 7).

    Chemistry

    ARTICLE SECTIONS
    Jump To
    Syntheses of analogues 11, 19, and 20 are summarized in Scheme 1. Alkylation of silyl protected 5-hexyn-1-ol(40)23 with 2-(9-bromononaloxy)tetrahydro-2H-pyran(41) in THF/HMPA gave 24 in good yield. Removal of the silyl protecting group, followed by semihydrogenation of the alkyne over catalytic P2-nickel, and substitution of the hydroxyl group for an azide under Mitsunobu conditions provided 25. Staudinger reduction of the azide and subsequent substitutive amidation of the crude amine with ethyl 2-(methylamino)-2-oxoacetate(42) gave 26 (71% over two steps). The tetrahydropyranyl group was uneventfully removed under acidic conditions, and the resulting alcohol was converted to either iodide 27 or bromide 28 under Appel-type conditions.(43) Substitution of the iodide in 27 with sodium sulfite, phenol 29,(44) or o-thiophenol gave 11, 19, and 20, respectively.
    Scheme 2 outlines the syntheses of analogues 12â15 and is representative of the approach utilized for 21 and 22. Briefly, bromide 28 was transformed into dimethylphosphonate 12 via a MichaelisâArbuzov reaction. Demethylation of the phosphonate esters using trimethylsilyl bromide afforded 13 after basification with aqueous sodium carbonate and SM-2 Bio-Bead (Bio-Rad) extraction. The mixed methyl/pivaloyloxymethyl (POM) ester 14 and di-POM ester 15 were obtained from 12 by substitution with POM iodide.
    Heterocyclic analogues 16, 17, and 18 were obtained from the common precursor, 30, that in turn was secured from bromide 28 via SN2 cyanation (Scheme 3). Following literature precedent,(45) 1,3-dipolar cycloaddition of 30 with hydrazoic acid, generated in situ from sodium azide and triethylamine hydrochloride, in dioxane provided 16 in moderate yield. Addition of hydroxylamine to 30 provided amidoxime 31, which was reacted with either thionyl chloride or 1,1â²-carbonyldiimidazole to furnish 17 and 18, respectively.
    The syntheses of 3 and 4 were accomplished using a more convergent synthetic strategy involving SuzukiâMiyaura coupling between vinyl iodide 35 or 36 and borane 39 (Scheme 4). The latter was generated in two steps via chemoselective In(OTf)3 mediated diazo OâH insertion(46) using commercial 6-hepten-1-ol (37) and ethyl diazoacetate followed by hydroboration of the resultant ether 38 with 9-BBN. Urea 35 and oxamide 36 were synthesized from the known alkyne 32(47) in four routine steps involving silver-mediated alkyne iodination to give 33, disiamylborane reduction, phthalimide deprotection affording vinyl iodide 34, and subsequent addition to ethyl isocyanate or acylation with ethyl (methylamino) (oxo) acetate, respectively. Pd-catalyzed cross-coupling led to 40 and 41. Final ester hydrolysis and acidification delivered 3 and 4, respectively.
    Finally, esters 5 and 6 as well as amides 7â10 were obtained from 2a, 2b, and 4, as described in Scheme 5. Routine EDCI/DCC couplings and ester hydrolysis under basic conditions provided the desired compounds.

    Conclusion

    ARTICLE SECTIONS
    Jump To
    The identification of 17(R),18(S)-epoxyeicosatetraenoic acid (EEQ) as the primary cytochrome P450 metabolite of EPA and the recognition of EEQâs powerful biological activities provided a cogent mechanistic foundation to help rationalize many of the cardiovascular benefits attributed to diets rich in fish oils. SAR studies in these laboratories elucidated the pharmacophore responsible for EEQâs antiarrhythmic effects and supported significant progress toward more drug-like analogues; however, these were still unsuitable for in vivo applications due to limited oral bioavailability and metabolic stability. Subsequent investigations as described herein of prodrug modifications, carboxylate bioisosteres, and carbon backbone substitutions identified the 3-oxa analogue 4 whose in vivo ADME/PK properties make it a potential candidate for further preclinical and clinical development.

    Chemical Syntheses

    General Information

    Nuclear magnetic resonance (NMR) spectra were recorded in CDCl3 with TMS as internal standard, unless otherwise stated. 1H NMR data are reported as follows: chemical shift (ppm), multiplicity (s = singlet, br s = broad singlet, d = doublet, t = triplet, q = quartet, app q = apparent quartet, qn = quintet, app qn = apparent quintet, m = multiplet), and coupling constant (Hz). Melting points were measured using an automated melting point apparatus and are uncorrected. Analytical thin layer chromatography (TLC) used EMD Chemicals TLC silica gel 60 F254 plates (0.040â0.063 mm) with visualization by UV light and/or KMnO4 or phosphomolybdic acid (PMA) solution followed by heating. All oxygen and/or moisture sensitive reactions were performed under an argon atmosphere using oven-dried glassware and anhydrous solvents. Extracts were dried over anhydrous Na2SO4 and filtered prior to removal of all volatiles under reduced pressure. Chromatographic purifications utilized preparative TLC or flash chromatography using commercial prepacked SiO2 columns on a CombiFlash Rf 200 chromatograph (Teledyne Isco). Unless otherwise noted, yields refer to isolated, purified material with spectral data consistent with assigned structures or, if known, were concordant with published data. Reagents were the highest commercial quality available and used without further purification, unless otherwise noted. POM-I was purchased from Enamine LLC, Monmouth Jct., NJ 08852 USA. Reaction solvents, except anhydrous CH3CN, were dried via passage under argon through activated, neutral alumina columns immediately prior to use. The purity of all test compounds was determined to be â¥95% by a combination of high-field 13C/1H NMR and LC-MS using a Luna C18(2) (3 Î¼m), 100 Ã 2 mm, and/or an Agilent ZORBAX Eclipse XDB-C18 (3.5 Î¼m), 50 Ã 2.1 mm, using a gradient of aq. ammonium acetate (50 mM) and CH3CN or MeOH.

    Measurement of Aqueous Solubility
    Each 17,18-EEQ analogue (2 mg) was weighed in a glass vial followed by the addition of deionized water and double-stoichiometric amounts of aq. NaOH (0.5 mL total volume). After stirring for 30 min at rt, potassium phosphate buffer (200 mM, 0.5 mL) was added to give a final pH of 7.4. Aliquots (100 Î¼L) were taken after 1 and 24 h of stirring continuously at rt using a magnetic stirrer and centrifuged for 5 min at 15,000 rpm. The supernatant was then analyzed for the amount of dissolved compound via RP-HPLC using a Nucleosil 100-5 C18 HD column (250 Ã 4 mm; Macherey-Nagel) and a linear solvent gradient ranging from acetonitrile/water/acetic acid (50:50:0.1, v/v/v) to acetonitrile/water/acetic acid (75:25:0.1) over 15 min at a flow rate of 1 mL/min. The compounds were detected at 210 nm using a diode array detector connected to the HPLC system. Retention times were 6.8 min (4), 8.5 min (16), and 11.3 min (2b).
    Determination of LogD Values
    Distribution coefficients (logD7.4 values) of analogues 2b, 4, and 16 were determined by the Institute of Organic Synthesis (Riga, Latvia) using an n-octanol/water system. 80 mM Tris/phosphoric acid buffer (pH 7.4) was used as the aqueous phase. After dissolving the respective analogue in Tris-buffer-saturated n-octanol (3.75 mg/mL), an equal volume of n-octanol-saturated Tris buffer was added, followed by vigorous shaking for 30 min and equilibration for 24 h at 25 Â°C. Aliquots were taken from the resulting n-octanol and aqueous phases and analyzed by HPLC with UV-detection using an Apollo C18 column (5 Î¼m, 4.6 Ã 150 mm; Hichrom) and a linear gradient from 40 to 95% acetonitrile in 0.1% H3PO4 over 15 min, at a flow rate of 1 mL/min. The analogues were detected at 210 nm. LogD7.4 values were calculated from the ratio of the peak areas of the analogues in the n-octanol versus aqueous phase chromatograms.
    Calculation of pKa Values
    The pKa values of analogues 2b, 4, and 16 were predicted using Marvin Sketch, version 19.9 (https://docs.chemaxon.com/display/docs/pKa+Plugin). For calculations, the following parameters were chosen: static acid/base prefix, i.e., the submitted ionic forms are converted to their neutral forms (adding or removing protons) and their pKa was then calculated; min basic pKa = â2; max acidic pKa = 16; and temperature = 298 K.
    NRCM Assay
    The biological activities of natural metabolites and synthetic analogues were determined using a bioassay essentially as described previously.(28) NRCMs were isolated from the hearts of 1â4 day old Wistar rats using the Neonatal Heart Dissociation Kit (#130-098-373, Miltenyi Biotech, Germany) and the Gentle MACS Dissociator (#130-093-235, Miltenyi Biotech, Germany). After removing fibroblasts by preplating, the isolated cardiomyocytes were cultured on the bottom of Falcon flasks (12.5 cm2; 1.1 Ã 106 cells/flask) in 2.5 mL of SM 20-I medium (Biochrom, #F9005) supplemented with 10% heat-inactivated newborn calf serum, 2 Î¼M fluorodeoxyuridine, 2 mM l-alanyl-l-glutamine, 0.5 Î¼M hydrocortisone, 100 U/mL penicillin, 100 Î¼g/mL streptomycin, and 5 Î¼M N-(methylsulfonyl)-2-(2-propynyloxy)-benzenehexanamide (MS-PPOH; Cayman Chemicals). MS-PPOH served to inhibit endogenous CYP-epoxyeicosanoid formation and was washed out by medium replacement 4 h before testing the exogenous compounds. Spontaneously beating cell clusters occurred after 5â7 days (120â150 beats/min). Beating rates were recorded using an IonOptix Myocyte Contractility System equipped with an inverted microscope (Leica DM IRB) and a CCD camera (Figure 8).
    Beating rates were determined for six to eight individual clusters before and 7 min after addition of the test substance. Based upon the difference between the basal and compound-modulated beating rate of the individual clusters, the chronotropic effects (changes in beats/min) were calculated and are given as means Â± SEM values; n = 18â40 clusters originating from at least three independent NRCM cultures. All test compounds were prepared as stock solutions in ethanol and tested at a final concentration of 30 nM. An aq. ethanol solution (0.025%) served as the vehicle control.
    Effect on sEH Activity
    Recombinant human sEH was purchased from Cayman Chemicals and diluted in incubation buffer containing 0.1% fatty-acid-free BSA. The hydrolase assays were performed at 30 Â°C in 300 Î¼L of 50 mM TrisâHCl buffer (pH 7.5) containing 0.01% fatty-acid-free BSA and 14,15-EET (10 Î¼M) as substrate. The compounds to be tested for inhibitory activity were added as ethanolic stock solutions to give a final concentration of 1 or 10 Î¼M. Ethanol (0.01%) served as the vehicle control. Reactions were started by adding human sEH (0.01 Î¼g) and stopped after 10 min with 15 Î¼L of 400 mM citric acid. The remaining substrate and its product (14,15-DHET) were extracted and analyzed by reversed-phase HPLC as described previously.(22)
    In Vitro ADME Properties
    Analogues 2b, 4, and 16 were analyzed for their stability in plasma, plasma protein binding, metabolic stability in hepatocytes, Caco-2 cell permeation, and CYP inhibition using the in vitro assays described below. These studies were performed by Pharmacelsus GmbH (SaarbrÃ¼cken, Germany; Project 2019OMC002). If not indicated otherwise, liquid chromatography-electrospray-tandem mass spectrometry (LC-ESI-MS/MS) was used for quantitative analysis. Chromatographic separations were performed on a Kinetex Phenyl-Hexyl analytical column (Phenomenex) using acetonitrile/water/0.1% formic acid gradients ranging from 5 to 97% of acetonitrile. 2b, 4, and 16 as assay-specific reference compounds were detected and quantified in the selected reaction monitoring mode using the following [M + H]+ to fragment ion transitions: 2b (m/z 355â262), 4 (m/z 357â311), 16 (m/z 379â277), propantheline (m/z 368â181), warfarin (m/z 309â251), 7-ethoxycoumarin (m/z 191â163), testosterone (m/z 289â97), atenolol (m/z 267â145), and erythromycin (m/z 734â576). Diazepam (m/z 285â193) served as an internal standard. Calibration standards were prepared by spiking the respective assay matrix, after adding acetonitrile and the internal standard, with the corresponding test compound.
    Plasma Stability
    Analogues 2b, 4, and 16 were incubated at a final concentration of 10 Î¼M with human plasma at 37 Â°C over 0, 15, 60, 120, and 240 min. All incubations were run in triplicate. For each time point, the incubations were started by adding 3 Î¼L of a 50-fold concentrated solution of the respective compound to 147 Î¼L of human plasma (pool from five donors, with lithium heparin as anticoagulant, purchased from Sera Lab International). The final solvent (DMSO) concentration was 0.5%. Positive controls were run in parallel using an esterase-cleavable reference compound (propantheline, N-isopropyl-N-methyl-N-{2-[(9H-xanthen-9-ylcarbonyl)oxy]ethyl}propan-2-aminium bromide). The incubations were stopped by adding 300 Î¼L of acetonitrile containing 1 Î¼M diazepam as an internal standard. After centrifugation (2200 Ã g, 10 min), the supernatant was diluted with an equal volume of PBS buffer and analyzed by LC-ESI-MS/MS as described above to determine the percentage of parental compound remaining. Analysis of the incubations with propantheline showed that this reference compound was almost completely degraded within 60 min of incubation (2.6% remaining compound).
    Plasma Protein Binding Assay
    Plasma protein binding of analogues 2b, 4, and 16 was analyzed using human plasma pooled from mixed gender with lithium heparin as anticoagulant (purchased from Sera Lab International). The assay was performed according to the modified ultrafiltration procedure, as described by Taylor and Harker.(54) The analogues and reference compounds were incubated with human plasma for 60 min at 37 Â°C in the dark at a final concentration of 10 Î¼M in the presence of 0.5% DMSO. Warfarin (10 Î¼M in the presence of 1% acetonitrile) served as a high binding positive control. All incubations were run in triplicate. After incubation, the samples were loaded onto ultrafiltration units (Ultracel-YM30, MWCO 30000 Da; Millipore, USA) and centrifuged at 6800 Ã g for 12 min. The obtained plasma retentates and filtrates were subjected to LC-ESI-MS/MS analysis as described above. For the reference compound warfarin, the calculated percentage of plasma protein binding was 99.2 Â± 0.2%.
    Stability with Primary Hepatocytes
    Metabolic stabilities of 2b, 4, and 16 were tested using primary human hepatocytes (five-donor pool, mixed gender, Bioreclamation IVT (Westbury, NY), #X008052). The cryopreserved hepatocytes were thawed according to the instructions of the supplier, and the incubations were performed in Williams Medium E (PAN Biotech) supplemented with 25 mM HEPES and 2 mM l-glutamine. The incubation samples contained 0.2 Ã 106 cells and the test compound at an initial concentration of 1 Î¼M in a total volume of 250 Î¼L. The final solvent (DMSO) concentration was 0.5%. The incubations were stopped after 0, 15, 60, 90, and 180 min by withdrawing a 200 Î¼L aliquot from the suspension cultures and adding an equal volume of acetonitrile containing 1 Î¼M diazepam as internal standard. After centrifugation (4800 Ã g, 5 min), the supernatant was diluted with an equal volume of water and analyzed by LC-ESI-MS/MS as described above. The amount of compound in the samples was expressed as the percentage of remaining compound compared to time point zero. These percentages were plotted against the corresponding time points. In vitro intrinsic clearance (Clint) and half-life (t1/2) were calculated using established formulas.(55) Positive control incubations with 7-ethoxycoumarin (5 Î¼M) showed that this reference substrate was rapidly degraded by the hepatocytes with a t1/2 value of 20.5 min and an in vitro Clint value of 43.22 Î¼L/min/106 cells. Negative controls showed that the compounds were stable in the incubation medium: 85.3 Â± 4% of 2b, 102 Â± 2% of 4, and 104 Â± 5% of 16 remained after 180 min in incubations performed without hepatocytes.
    Metabolism by Human Liver Microsomes
    Human liver microsomes were purchased from Corning/BD Biosciences (mixed gender, pooled from 150 donors, lot 38292). Reactions were performed in 100 mM phosphate buffer pH 7.4 containing 2 mM MgCl2, 0.5 mg microsomal protein/mL, and the test compound at a concentration of 1 Î¼M. The final solvent (DMSO) concentration was 0.5%. Reactions were started by adding NADPH (1 mM final concentration) and stopped after 0, 10, 30, or 60 min of incubation at 37 Â°C by adding an equal volume of acetonitrile. Subsequent LC-MS analysis was performed in single ion monitoring mode for detecting the molecular ions [M + H]+ of the respective parental compound (2b at m/z 355.26; 4 at 357.24; 16 at 379.28; oleic acid at 281.25) and potential metabolites resulting from monooxygenation (molecular ion +16) and epoxidation followed by epoxide hydrolysis (molecular ion +34).
    Caco-2 Permeation Assay
    A bidirectional transwell assay was used to determine the transport of 2b, 4, and 16 across Caco-2 cell monolayers. The cells were seeded on permeable membrane inserts with a surface of 1.1 cm2 and grown to confluence over 21 days in DMEM supplemented with 10% fetal bovine serum and nonessential amino acids. After washing twice with Hanks balanced salt solution (HBSS), the cells were equilibrated for 20 min at 37 Â°C using HBSS containing 0.5 mM MgCl2, 1.25 mM CaCl2, and 25 mM glucose supplemented with 20 mM MES (pH 6.5) as apical buffer or with 20 mM HEPES (pH 7.4) as basolateral buffer. The assay was started by replacing the apical or basolateral buffer by the corresponding buffer containing the test compound at a final concentration of 10 Î¼M in the presence of 0.5% DMSO. The Transwell plates were then incubated at 37 Â°C at 20 rpm in a shaking water bath, and samples were taken from the basolateral or apical side after 15, 45, and 90 min. The samples were mixed with a 0.5-fold volume of acetonitrile containing 1 Î¼M diazepam as internal standard, cleared by centrifugation, and subjected to LC-ESI-MS/MS as described above. All assays were run in triplicate. Reference compounds analyzed in parallel showed apical to basolateral (a-b) and basolateral to apical (b-a) permeation rates as expected for a substrate with high (testosterone: 32.5 in a-b direction and 58.9 Ã 106 cm/s in b-a direction) and low (atenelol: 0.2 in a-b direction and 0.1 Ã 106 cm/s in b-a direction) permeability. A high efflux rate was observed with erythromycin (0.35 in a-b direction and 10.4 Ã 106 cm/s in b-a direction), confirming functional expression of P-glycoprotein in the cultured Caco-2 cells.
    CYP Inhibition Assay
    Human P450 inhibition screening kits were purchased from BD Gentest (Corning, NY) and used to test 2b, 4, and 16 for potential inhibition of CYP1A2, CYP2B6, CYP2C8, CYP2C9, CYP2C19, CYP2D6, and CYP3A4A. The assays were performed in black 96-well plates according to the manufacturerâs instructions. To obtain concentrationâresponse curves, 2b, 4, and 16 were tested at eight different final concentrations ranging from 0.023 to 50 Î¼M. All reactions were performed in triplicate. The reactions were stopped by the addition of stop solution (75 Î¼L/well) containing 60% acetonitrile and 40% 0.1 M Tris, pH 9.0. The fluorescence was measured using a Wallac Victor fluorescence plate reader. The wavelengths for excitation and emission of the individual fluorescent metabolites varied depending on the probe substrate for measuring individual CYP enzyme activities. Solvent controls (100% enzyme activity, no inhibition) and negative controls (blank values) were run in parallel. Positive controls with reference inhibitors yielded the following IC50 values (in Î¼M): CYP1A2/furafylline (5.7), CYP2B6/tranylcypromine (6.2), CYP2C8/quercetin (7.0), CYP2C9/sulphaphenazole (0.28), CYP2C19/tranylcypromine(2.8), CYP2D6/quinidine (0.01), and CYP3A4/ketoconazole (0.04).
    Inhibition of CYP2J2 Catalyzed EPA Metabolism
    Supersomes containing recombinant human CYP2J2, NADPH-CYP reductase, and cytochrome b5 were purchased from Corning (product # 456264). Reactions were performed in 100 Î¼L of 100 mM potassium phosphate buffer (pH 7.4) containing 10 pmol of CYP2J2 and EPA (10 Î¼M) as substrate. CYP2J2 was preincubated with the substrate for 5 min at 37 Â°C in the presence of vehicle (0.5% DMSO) alone or the potential inhibitor to be tested (analogues 2b, 4, and 16 and the reference compounds amiodarone, dronedarone, and terfenadine) at final concentrations of 1, 10, or 50 Î¼M. Reactions were started with NADPH (final concentration of 1 mM) and terminated after 15 min by adding 5 Î¼L of 400 mM citric acid. Subsequent extraction and LC-MS/MS analysis of the reaction products were performed as described previously (Arnold et al. 2010). The EPA epoxygenase activity of recombinant CYP2J2 was calculated from the sum of regioisomeric epoxyeicosatetraenoic acids (8,9-, 11,12-, 14,15-, and 17,18-EEQ) and their corresponding vicinal diols (vic-dihydoxyeicosatetraenoic acids; DiHETEs) produced in the incubations. No product formation occurred if NADPH was omitted from the reaction mixtures.
    Pharmacokinetics in Animal Models
    Pharmacokinetic studies were done at Pharmacelsus GmbH (Science Park 2, 66123 SaarbrÃ¼cken, Germany, Project 2017OMC003). Briefly, adult male SpragueâDawley rats (RjHan:SD) were purchased from Janvier Laboratories (France) and catheterized 3 days before test compound administration. All animals were fasted overnight before test compound administration and fed again 4 h post dose. Water was provided ad libitum throughout the study. Test compound formulations were administered intravenously via a vascular access harness [1 mg/kg bw in DMSO/PEG400 (1:4)] or by oral gavage (3 mg/kg bw in 0.5% methylcellulose), while the rats were manually restrained. Prior to the first blood sampling, animals were connected to a tether system that allows for blood sampling in the freely moving rat. At each of the designated time points, namely, 0.083 (only IV), 0.25, 0.5, 1, 2, 4, 8, 24, and 36 (only oral) h after application, 100 Î¼L of blood was collected from the jugular catheter in tubes containing Li heparin, stored on ice, and subsequently centrifuged at 3000 Ã g for 10 min at 4 Â°C (Heraeus Biofuge fresco). The plasma was prepared within 45 min after sampling and was kept at â20 Â°C until assayed. All samples were stored at â20 Â°C until processing by LC-MS analysis.
    Rat Model of Ventricular Arrhythmia
    All animal procedures were approved by the Landesamt fÃ¼r Gesundheit and Soziales (LaGeSo, Berlin, Germany) for the use of laboratory animals (Approval number: G0283/12) and followed the âPrinciples of Laboratory Animal Careââ (NIH publication No. 86-23, revised 1985) as well as the current version of German Law on the Protection of Animals. Male Wistar rats weighing 220â250 g were randomized to receive an i.v. bolus injection of analogue 5 (100 Î¼g in 300 Î¼L of 0.9% NaCl) or only 300 Î¼L of 0.9% NaCl as the vehicle control 10 min before induction of myocardial infarction. Animals were anesthetized by i.p. injection of ketamin/xylazin (80 mg of ketamin/12 mg of xylazin, Sigma-Aldrich) and analogue 5 or vehicle control were injected into the vena subclavia. Continuous monitoring of surface ECG was started (EPTracer, Netherlands) and maintained until the end of the study (45 min after induction of myocardial infarction). After recording the basal ECG, myocardial infarction was induced by ligation of the left anterior descending artery (LAD). Forty-five min after LAD occlusion, the animals were sacrificed. Ventricular tachycardia (VT) burden was calculated as the sum of all sustained arrhythmic events (VT > 1.5 s) originating from the myocardium during the observational period of 45 min after LAD occlusion. To quantify the severity of ventricular arrhythmias, arrhythmic events were classified into premature ventricular contractions (PVC), couplet, triplet, VT < 1.5 s, and VT > 1.5 s. Each class was factorized by an increasing severity index of 1â5: PVC Ã 1, couplets Ã 2, triplets Ã 3, VT < 1.5 s Ã 4, VT > 1.5 s Ã 5).

      The pathology and treatment of cardiac arrhythmias: focus on atrial fibrillation
      Schmidt Constanze; Kisselbach Jana; Schweizer Patrick A; Katus Hugo A; Thomas Dierk
      Vascular health and risk management (2011), 7 (), 193-202 ISSN:.
      Atrial fibrillation (AF) is the most frequently encountered sustained cardiac arrhythmia in clinical practice and a major cause of morbidity and mortality. Effective treatment of AF still remains an unmet medical need. Treatment of AF is based on drug therapy and ablative strategies. Antiarrhythmic drug therapy is limited by a relatively high recurrence rate and proarrhythmic side effects. Catheter ablation suppresses paroxysmal AF in the majority of patients without structural heart disease but is more difficult to achieve in patients with persistent AF or with concomitant cardiac disease. Stroke is a potentially devastating complication of AF, requiring anticoagulation that harbors the risk of bleeding. In search of novel treatment modalities, targeted pharmacological treatment and gene therapy offer the potential for greater selectivity than conventional small-molecule or interventional approaches. This paper summarizes the current understanding of molecular mechanisms underlying AF. Established drug therapy and interventional treatment of AF is reviewed, and emerging clinical and experimental therapeutic approaches are highlighted.

      5
      Adverse effects of class I antiarrhythmic drugs
      Caron, Jacques; Libersa, Christian
      Drug Safety (1997), 17 (1), 8-36CODEN: DRSAEA; ISSN:0114-5916. (Adis)
      A review with 366 refs. Class I antiarrhythmic drugs are characterized by their ability to block the fast inward sodium current in cardiac muscle tissue. However, at the same time, they can be responsible for various effects involving other organs and systems. Although some of these effects can be helpful in specific situations, most of them, such as their pro-arrhythmic propensity, are deleterious. Some of the adverse effects of class I antiarrhythmic drugs are directly linked to sodium-channel blockade (conduction disorders, hemodynamic perturbations, and digestive and neurol. effects), while others are linked to other specific pharmacol. properties (e.g. atropinic, or Î±- or Î²-adrenergic blockade) or to nonspecific properties (idiosyncratic hypersensitivity, and hematol., dermatol. or hepatic reactions). Other adverse effects are assocd. with complex interactions between class I antiarrhythmics and individual predisposing factors, trigger mechanisms and physiol. factors (including concomitant drug treatment). These numerous variations and interactions within a specific environment and underlying disorder might be of pharmacol. or/and pharmacokinetic origin, making anal. of the true liability of the class I drugs very difficult when adverse effects occur.

      n-3 fatty acids, ventricular arrhythmia-related events, and fatal myocardial infarction in postmyocardial infarction patients with diabetes
      Kromhout, Daan; Geleijnse, Johanna M.; de Goede, Janette; Oude Griep, Linda M.; Mulder, Barbara J. M.; de Boer, Menko-Jan; Deckers, Jaap W.; Boersma, Eric; Zock, Peter L.; Giltay, Erik J.
      Diabetes Care (2011), 34 (12), 2515-2520CODEN: DICAD2; ISSN:0149-5992. (American Diabetes Association, Inc.)
      OBJECTIVE-We carried out a secondary anal. in high-risk patients with a previous myocardial infarction (Ml) and diabetes in the Alpha Omega Trial. We tested the hypothesis that in these patients an increased intake of the n-3 fatty acids eicosapentaenoic acid (EPA), docosahexaenoic acid (DHA), and Î±-linolenic acid (ALA) will reduce the incidence of ventricular arrhythmias and fatal MI. RESEARCH DESIGN AND METHODS-A subgroup of 1,014 post-MI patients with diabetes aged 60-80 years was randomly allocated to receive one of four trial margarines, three with an addnl. amt. of n-3 fatty acids and one placebo for 40 mo. The end points were ventricular arrhythmia-related events and fatal Ml. The data were analyzed according to the intention-to-treat principle, using multivariable Cox proportional hazards models. RESULTS-The patients consumed on av. 18.6 g of margarine per day, which resulted in an addnl. intake of 223 mg EPA plus 149 mg DHA and/or 1.9 g ALA in the active treatment groups. During follow-up, 29 patients developed a ventricular arrhythmia-related events and 27 had a fatal MI. Compared with placebo patients, the EPA-DHA plus ALA group experienced less ventricular arrhythmia-related events (hazard ratio 0.16; 95% CI 0.04-0.69). These n-3 fatty acids also reduced the combined end-point ventricular arrhythmia-related events and fatal MI (0.28; 0.11-0.71). CONCLUSIONS-Our results suggest that low-dose supplementation of n-3 fatty acids exerts a protective effect against ventricular arrhythmia-related events in post-MI patients with diabetes.

      Prevention of fatal cardiac arrhythmias by polyunsaturated fatty acids
      Kang, Jing X.; Leaf, Alexander
      American Journal of Clinical Nutrition (2000), 71 (1, Suppl.), 202S-207SCODEN: AJCNAC; ISSN:0002-9165. (American Society for Clinical Nutrition)
      A review with 33 refs. In animal studies, and probably in humans, dietary n-3 polyunsatd. fatty acids (PUFA) can prevent fatal ischemia-induced cardiac arrhythmias. The n-3 PUFA can also prevent heart arrhythmias in surgically prepd. conscious exercising dogs. The mechanism of the antiarrhythmic action of n-3 PUFA has been studied by the authors in spontaneously contracting cultured cardiac myocytes of neonatal rats. Adding arrhythmogenic toxins (ouabain, high Ca2+, lysophosphatidylcholine, Î²-adrenergic agonist, acylcarnitine, Ca ionophore) to the myocyte perfusate caused tachycardia, contracture, and fibrillation of the cultured myocytes. Adding eicosapentaenoic acid (EPA; 5-15 Î¼mol/L) to the superfusate before adding the toxins prevented the expected tachyarrhythmias. When the arrhythmias were first induced, adding EPA to the superfusate terminated the arrhythmias. This antiarrhythmic action occurred with dietary n-3 and n-6 PUFA; satd. fatty acids and the monounsatd. oleic acid had no such activity. Arachidonic acid (AA; C20:4n-6) effects were anomalous because in 1/3 of the tests it provoked severe arrhythmias due to cyclooxygenase metabolites of AA. When cyclooxygenase inhibitors were added with AA, the antiarrhythmic effect was like those of EPA and DHA. The action of the n-3 and n-6 PUFA is to stabilize elec. every myocyte in the heart by increasing the elec. stimulus required to elicit an action potential by â¼50% and prolonging the relative refractory time by â¼150%. These electrophysiol. effects result from an action of free PUFA to modulate sodium and calcium currents in the myocytes. PUFA also modulate sodium and calcium channels and have anticonvulsant activity in brain cells.

      Upstream Treatment of Atrial Fibrillation with n-3 Polyunsaturated Fatty Acids: Myth or Reality?
      Orso Francesco; Fabbri Gianna; Maggioni Aldo Pietro
      Arrhythmia & electrophysiology review (2015), 4 (3), 163-8 ISSN:2050-3369.
      Atrial fibrillation (AF) is the most common sustained arrhythmia in adults and is associated with an increased risk of fatal and non-fatal events. Antiarrhythmic drugs provide limited protection against AF recurrence and have a poor safety profile. Several mechanisms have been proven to be involved in AF, e.g. inflammation, oxidative stress, fibrosis and ischaemia. Prevention of AF with interventions that target these mechanisms has emerged as a result of experimental studies suggesting the use of upstream therapies. Long chain n-3 polyunsaturated fatty acids (n-3 PUFA) have multiple effects on cardiac electrophysiology, and epidemiological studies on fish oil suggest a possible use of n-3 PUFA in AF prevention. Several randomised clinical trials have been designed to evaluate the efficacy of n-3 PUFA in preventing AF. In this review, we report the conflicting results of these trials in two different clinical settings: recurrence in patients with history of AF and development of post-operative AF in patient undergoing cardiac surgery.

      von Schacky, C. Omega-3 Fatty Acids: Anti-Arrhythmic, Pro-Arrhythmic, or Both?. Front. Physiol. 2012, 3, 88, Â DOI: 10.3389/fphys.2012.00088
      [Crossref], [PubMed], [CAS],Â Google Scholar
      13
      Omega-3 Fatty acids: anti-arrhythmic, pro-arrhythmic, or both?
      von Schacky C
      Frontiers in physiology (2012), 3 (), 88 ISSN:.
      This review focuses on developments after 2008, when the topic was last reviewed by the author. Pertinent publications were found by medline searches and in the author's personal data base. Prevention of atrial fibrillation (AF) was investigated in a number of trials, sparked by one positive report on the effects of eicosapentaenoic acid (EPA) and docosahexaenoic acid (DHA), considerations of upstream therapy, data from electrophysiologic laboratories and animal experiments. If EPA + DHA prevent postoperative AF, the effect is probably smaller than initially expected. The same is probably true for maintenance of sinus rhythm after cardioversion and for new-onset AF. Larger trials are currently ongoing. Prevention of ventricular arrhythmias was studied in carriers of an implanted cardioverter-defibrillator, with no clear results. This might have been due to a broad definition of the primary endpoint, including any ventricular arrhythmia and any action of the device. Epidemiologic studies support the contention that high levels of EPA + DHA prevent sudden cardiac death (SCD). However, since SCD is a rare occurrence, it is difficult to conduct an adequately powered trial. In patients with congestive heart failure, EPA + DHA reduced total mortality and rehospitalizations, but not SCD or presumed arrhythmic death. Of three trials in patients after a myocardial infarction, two were inadequately powered, and in one, the dose might have been too low. Taken together, while epidemiologic studies support an inverse relation between EPA + DHA and occurrence of SCD or arrhythmic death, demonstrating this effect in intervention trials remained elusive so far. A pro-arrhythmic effect of EPA + DHA has not been seen in intervention studies, and results of epidemiologic and animal studies also rather argue against such an effect. A different, and probably more productive, perspective is provided by a standardized analytical assessment of a person's status in EPA + DHA by use of the omega-3 index, EPA + DHA in red cell fatty acids. In populations with a high omega-3 index, SCD is rare. Intervention trials can become more effective by including a low omega-3 index into the inclusion criteria, thus creating a study population more likely to demonstrate an effect of EPA + DHA. This is especially relevant in case of rare endpoints, like new-onset AF or SCD.

      CYP-eicosanoids-A new link between omega-3 fatty acids and cardiac disease?
      Westphal, Christina; Konkel, Anne; Schunck, Wolf-Hagen
      Prostaglandins & Other Lipid Mediators (2011), 96 (1-4), 99-108CODEN: POLMFL; ISSN:1098-8823. (Elsevier)
      A review. Fish oil omega-3 fatty acids such as eicosapentaenoic acid (EPA) and docosahexaenoic acid (DHA) protect against arrhythmia and sudden cardiac death by largely unknown mechanisms. Recent in vitro and in vivo studies demonstrate that arachidonic acid (AA) metabolizing cytochrome P 450-(CYP) enzymes accept EPA and DHA as efficient alternative substrates. This hypothesis is discussed in the context of recent findings that revealed CYP-eicosanoid mediated mechanisms in cardiac ischemia-reperfusion injury and maladaptive cardiac hypertrophy.

      Therapeutic potential of omega-3 fatty acid-derived epoxyeicosanoids in cardiovascular and inflammatory diseases
      Schunck, Wolf-Hagen; Konkel, Anne; Fischer, Robert; Weylandt, Karsten-Henrich
      Pharmacology & Therapeutics (2018), 183 (), 177-204CODEN: PHTHDT; ISSN:0163-7258. (Elsevier)
      Numerous benefits have been attributed to dietary long-chain omega-3 polyunsatd. fatty acids (n-3 LC-PUFAs), including protection against cardiac arrhythmia, triglyceride-lowering, amelioration of inflammatory, and neurodegenerative disorders. This review covers recent findings indicating that a variety of these beneficial effects are mediated by "omega-3 epoxyeicosanoids", a class of novel n-3 LC-PUFA-derived lipid mediators, which are generated via the cytochrome P 450 (CYP) epoxygenase pathway. CYP enzymes, previously identified as arachidonic acid (20:4n-6; AA) epoxygenases, accept eicosapentaenoic acid (20:5n-3; EPA) and docosahexaenoic acid (22:6n-3; DHA), the major fish oil n-3 LC-PUFAs, as efficient alternative substrates. In humans and rodents, dietary EPA/DHA supplementation causes a profound shift of the endogenous CYP-eicosanoid profile from AA- to EPA- and DHA-derived metabolites, increasing, in particular, the plasma and tissue levels of 17,18-epoxyeicosatetraenoic acid (17,18-EEQ) and 19,20-epoxydocosapentaenoic acid (19,20-EDP). Based on preclin. studies, these omega-3 epoxyeicosanoids display cardioprotective, vasodilatory, anti-inflammatory, and anti-allergic properties that contribute to the beneficial effects of n-3 LC-PUFAs in diverse disease conditions ranging from cardiac disease, bronchial disorders, and intraocular neovascularization, to allergic intestinal inflammation and inflammatory pain. Increasing evidence also suggests that background nutrition as well as genetic and disease state-related factors could limit the response to EPA/DHA-supplementation by reducing the formation and/or enhancing the degrdn. of omega-3 epoxyeicosanoids. Recently, metabolically robust synthetic analogs mimicking the biol. activities of 17,18-EEQ have been developed. These drug candidates may overcome limitations of dietary EPA/DHA supplementation and provide novel options for the treatment of cardiovascular and inflammatory diseases.

      Dietary n-3 Polyunsaturated Fatty Acids and Direct Renin Inhibition Improve Electrical Remodeling in a Model of High Human Renin Hypertension
      Fischer, Robert; Dechend, Ralf; Qadri, Fatimunnisa; Markovic, Marija; Feldt, Sandra; Herse, Florian; Park, Joon-Keun; Gapelyuk, Andrej; Schwarz, Ines; Zacharzowsky, Udo B.; Plehm, Ralph; Safak, Erdal; Heuser, Arnd; Schirdewan, Alexander; Luft, Friedrich C.; Schunck, Wolf-Hagen; Muller, Dominik N.
      Hypertension (2008), 51 (2, Pt. 2), 540-546CODEN: HPRTDN; ISSN:0194-911X. (Lippincott Williams & Wilkins)
      We compared the effect n-3 polyunsatd. fatty acids (PUFAs) with direct renin inhibition on electrophysiol. remodeling in angiotensin II-induced cardiac injury. We treated double-transgenic rats expressing the human renin and angiotensinogen genes (dTGRs) from week 4 to 7 with n-3 PUFA ethyl-esters (Omacor; 25-g/kg diet) or a direct renin inhibitor (aliskiren; 3 mg/kg per day). Sprague-Dawley rats were controls. We performed electrocardiog., magnetocardiog., and programmed elec. stimulation. Dietary n-3 PUFAs increased the cardiac content of eicosapentaenoic and docosahexaenoic acid. At week 7, mortality in dTGRs was 31%, whereas none of the n-3 PUFA- or aliskiren-treated dTGRs died. Aliskiren-treated dTGRs and Sprague-Dawley rats were normotensive (110 Â± 3 and 119 Â± 6 mm Hg, resp.). Both n-3 PUFA-treated and untreated dTGRs showed cardiac hypertrophy and increased atrial natriuretic peptide levels. Prolonged QRS and QTc intervals and increased T-wave dispersion in dTGRs were reduced by n-3 PUFAs or aliskiren. Both treatments reduced arrhythmia induction from 75% in dTGRs to 17% vs. 0% in Sprague-Dawley rats. Macrophage infiltration and fibrosis were reduced by n-3 PUFAs and aliskiren. Connexin 43, a mediator of intermyocyte conduction, was redistributed to the lateral cell membranes in dTGRs. n-3 PUFAs and aliskiren restored normal localization to the intercalated disks. Thus, n-3 PUFAs and aliskiren improved elec. remodeling, arrhythmia induction, and connexin 43 expression, despite a 70-mm Hg difference in blood pressure and the development of cardiac hypertrophy.

      Arachidonic Acid-metabolizing Cytochrome P450 Enzymes are Targets of Ï-3 Fatty Acids
      Arnold, Cosima; Markovic, Marija; Blossey, Katrin; Wallukat, Gerd; Fischer, Robert; Dechend, Ralf; Konkel, Anne; von Schacky, Clemens; Luft, Friedrich C.; Muller, Dominik N.; Rothe, Michael; Schunck, Wolf-Hagen
      Journal of Biological Chemistry (2010), 285 (43), 32720-32733CODEN: JBCHA3; ISSN:0021-9258. (American Society for Biochemistry and Molecular Biology)
      Eicosapentaenoic acid (EPA) and docosahexaenoic acid (DHA) protect against cardiovascular disease by largely unknown mechanisms. We tested the hypothesis that EPA and DHA may compete with arachidonic acid (AA) for the conversion by cytochrome P 450 (CYP) enzymes, resulting in the formation of alternative, physiol. active, metabolites. Renal and hepatic microsomes, as well as various CYP isoforms, displayed equal or elevated activities when metabolizing EPA or DHA instead of AA. CYP2C/2J isoforms converting AA to epoxyeicosatrienoic acids (EETs) preferentially epoxidized the Ï-3 double bond and thereby produced 17,18-epoxyeicosatetraenoic (17,18-EEQ) and 19,20-epoxydocosapentaenoic acid (19,20-EDP) from EPA and DHA. We found that these Ï-3 epoxides are highly active as antiarrhythmic agents, suppressing the Ca2+-induced increased rate of spontaneous beating of neonatal rat cardiomyocytes, at low nanomolar concns. CYP4A/4F isoforms Ï-hydroxylating AA were less regioselective toward EPA and DHA, catalyzing predominantly Ï- and Ï minus 1 hydroxylation. Rats given dietary EPA/DHA supplementation exhibited substantial replacement of AA by EPA and DHA in membrane phospholipids in plasma, heart, kidney, liver, lung, and pancreas, with less pronounced changes in the brain. The changes in fatty acids were accompanied by concomitant changes in endogenous CYP metabolite profiles (e.g. altering the EET/EEQ/EDP ratio from 87:0:13 to 27:18:55 in the heart). These results demonstrate that CYP enzymes efficiently convert EPA and DHA to novel epoxy and hydroxy metabolites that could mediate some of the beneficial cardiovascular effects of dietary Ï-3 fatty acids.

      Biochemical and molecular properties of the cytochrome P450 arachidonic acid monooxygenases
      Capdevila, Jorge H.; Falck, John R.
      Prostaglandins & Other Lipid Mediators (2002), 68-69 (), 325-344CODEN: POLMFL; ISSN:1098-8823. (Elsevier Science Inc.)
      A review. The cytochrome P 450 (P 450) arachidonic acid (AA) monooxygenase metabolizes the fatty acid to a series of epoxy- and hydroxy-acid derivs. Catalytic turnover requires NADPH, and requires the redox-coupled activation and cleavage of diat. oxygen, and the delivery of an active form of at. oxygen to ground state carbon atoms. Past and present advances in P 450 biochem. and mol. biol. are beginning to provide a description of the P 450 isoform specificity of AA bioactivation, and the mechanisms of action and physiol. relevance of the P 450 metabolites. The demonstration of the endogenous biosynthesis of many of these metabolites has established the P 450 pathway as an important route for AA bioactivation, and has begun to uncover new and important functional roles for this enzyme system in cell and organ physiol.

      Eicosapentaenoic acid metabolism by cytochrome P450 enzymes of the CYP2C subfamily
      Barbosa-Sicard, Eduardo; Markovic, Marija; Honeck, Horst; Christ, Baghat; Muller, Dominik N.; Schunck, Wolf-Hagen
      Biochemical and Biophysical Research Communications (2005), 329 (4), 1275-1281CODEN: BBRCA9; ISSN:0006-291X. (Elsevier)

      CYP2J2 overexpression protects against arrhythmia susceptibility in cardiac hypertrophy
      Westphal, Christina; Spallek, Bastian; Konkel, Anne; Marko, Lajos; Qadri, Fatimunnisa; DeGraff, Laura M.; Schubert, Carola; Bradbury, J. Alyce; Regitz-Zagrosek, Vera; Falck, John R.; Zeldin, Darryl C.; Mueller, Dominik N.; Schunck, Wolf-Hagen; Fischer, Robert
      PLoS One (2013), 8 (8), e73490CODEN: POLNCL; ISSN:1932-6203. (Public Library of Science)
      Maladaptive cardiac hypertrophy predisposes one to arrhythmia and sudden death. These results demonstrate that enhanced cardiac EET biosynthesis protects against elec. remodeling, ventricular tachyarrhythmia, and atrial fibrillation susceptibility during maladaptive cardiac hypertrophy.

      Dietary omega-3 fatty acids modulate the eicosanoid profile in man primarily via the CYP-epoxygenase pathway
      Fischer, Robert; Konkel, Anne; Mehling, Heidrun; Blossey, Katrin; Gapelyuk, Andrej; Wessel, Niels; von Schacky, Clemens; Dechend, Ralf; Muller, Dominik N.; Rothe, Michael; Luft, Friedrich C.; Weylandt, Karsten; Schunck, Wolf-Hagen
      Journal of Lipid Research (2014), 55 (6), 1150-1164CODEN: JLPRAW; ISSN:0022-2275. (American Society for Biochemistry and Molecular Biology, Inc.)
      Cytochrome P 450 (CYP)-dependent metabolites of arachidonic acid (AA) contribute to the regulation of cardiovascular function. CYP enzymes also accept EPA and DHA to yield more potent vasodilatory and potentially anti-arrhythmic metabolites, suggesting that the endogenous CYP-eicosanoid profile can be favorably shifted by dietary omega-3 fatty acids. To test this hypothesis, 20 healthy volunteers were treated with an EPA/DHA supplement and analyzed for concomitant changes in the circulatory and urinary levels of AA-, EPA-, and DHA-derived metabolites produced by the cyclooxygenase-, lipoxygenase (LOX)-, and CYP-dependent pathways. Raising the Omega-3 Index from about four to eight primarily resulted in a large increase of EPA-derived CYP-dependent epoxy-metabolites followed by increases of EPA- and DHA-derived LOX-dependent monohydroxy-metabolites including the precursors of the resolvin E and D families; resolvins themselves were not detected. The metabolite/precursor fatty acid ratios indicated that CYP epoxygenases metabolized EPA with an 8.6-fold higher efficiency and DHA with a 2.2-fold higher efficiency than AA. Effects on leukotriene, prostaglandin E, prostacyclin, and thromboxane formation remained rather weak. We propose that CYP-dependent epoxy-metabolites of EPA and DHA may function as mediators of the vasodilatory and cardioprotective effects of omega-3 fatty acids and could serve as biomarkers in clin. studies investigating the cardiovascular effects of EPA/DHA supplementation.

      Cytochrome P450 enzymes in the bioactivation of polyunsaturated fatty acids and their role in cardiovascular disease
      Westphal, Christina; Konkel, Anne; Schunck, Wolf-Hagen
      Advances in Experimental Medicine and Biology (2015), 851 (Monooxygenase, Peroxidase and Peroxygenase Properties and Mechanisms of Cytochrome P450), 151-187CODEN: AEMBAP; ISSN:2214-8019. (Springer)
      Various members of the cytochrome P 450 (CYP) superfamily have the capacity of metabolizing omega-6 and omega-3 polyunsatd. fatty acids (n-6 and n-3 PUFAs). In most mammalian tissues, CYP2C and CYP2J enzymes are the major PUFA epoxygenases, whereas CYP4A and CYP4F subfamily members function as PUFA hydroxylases. The individual CYP enzymes differ in their substrate specificities as well as regio- and stereoselectivities and thus produce distinct sets of epoxy and/or hydroxy metabolites, collectively termed CYP eicosanoids. Nutrition has a major impact on the endogenous CYP-eicosanoid profile. "Western diets" rich in n-6 PUFAs result in a predominance of arachidonic acid-derived metabolites, whereas marine foodstuffs rich in n-3 PUFAs shift the profile to eicosapentaenoic and docosahexaenoic acid-derived metabolites. In general, CYP eicosanoids are formed as second messengers of numerous hormones, growth factors and cytokines regulating cardiovascular and renal function, and a variety of other physiol. processes. Imbalances in the formation of individual CYP eicosanoids are linked to the development of hypertension, myocardial infarction, maladaptive cardiac hypertrophy, acute kidney injury, stroke and inflammatory disorders. The underlying mechanisms are increasingly understood and may provide novel targets for the prevention and treatment of these disease states. Suitable pharmacol. agents are underdevelopment and first proofs of concept have been obtained in animal models.

      Prodrugs of Phosphates and Phosphonates
      Hecker, Scott J.; Erion, Mark D.
      Journal of Medicinal Chemistry (2008), 51 (8), 2328-2345CODEN: JMCMAR; ISSN:0022-2623. (American Chemical Society)
      A review. The field of prodrugs for phosph(on)ates has evolved considerably in the past decade. There are now several approaches available to the researcher who finds that a phosphonate moiety achieves the right balance of properties in the lead series, yet needs a prodrug for oral delivery. The overall best choice in this situation, taking into account stability, byproduct toxicity, avoidance of stereoisomers, and validation in humans, appears to be the diamide class. Given its relatively recent appearance, there are few examples to date, but it is expected that phosphonic diamides will play an increasingly important role as phosphonate prodrugs going forward. For drugs given at low to moderate doses, where risks assocd. with the byproduct formaldehyde are low, acyloxyalkyl prodrugs will continue to find application. If selective targeting to the liver is desired, HepDirect prodrugs are the clear choice, whereas aryl amidates (and possibly diamides) offer the potential for targeting to lymphatic tissue. The increasing availability of prodrug options should encourage the practicing medicinal chemist to explore phosphonic acids in the course of lead development.

      Karkhanis, Aneesh; Lam, Hui Yuan; Venkatesan, Gopalakrishnan; Koh, Siew Kwan; Chai, Christina Li Lin; Zhou, Lei; Hong, Yanjun; Kojodjojo, Pipin; Chan, Eric Chun Yong

      Chiral lipidomics of monoepoxy and monohydroxy metabolites derived from long-chain polyunsaturated fatty acids
      Blum, Maximilian; Dogan, Inci; Karber, Mirjam; Rothe, Michael; Schunck, Wolf-Hagen

      Influence of heterocyclic and oxime-containing farnesol analogs on quorum sensing and pathogenicity in Candida albicans
      Shchepin, Roman; Navarathna, Dhammika H. M. L. P.; Dumitru, Raluca; Lippold, Shane; Nickerson, Kenneth W.; Dussault, Patrick H.
      Bioorganic & Medicinal Chemistry (2008), 16 (4), 1842-1848CODEN: BMECEP; ISSN:0968-0896. (Elsevier Ltd.)
      A series of synthetic mols. combining a geranyl backbone with a heterocyclic or oxime head group are quorum-sensing mols. that block the yeast to mycelium transition in the dimorphic fungus Candida albicans. A no. of the analogs have an IC50 â¤ 10 Î¼M, a level of potency essentially identical to the natural quorum sensing signal, the sesquiterpene farnesol. The results offer the first example of highly potent synthetic fungal quorum-sensing mols., and provide the first evidence for the ability to decouple quorum sensing and virulence.

The retentate from exptl. and control plasma samples were mixed back into the filtrate of the partner sample. The resulting regenerated plasma samples, one representing the exptl. filtrate and one representing the exptl. retentate, were then analyzed by LC/MS/MS. Varying degrees of NSB were demonstrated with a no. of corticosteroids, and this effect was eliminated using the modified method. Validation using a panel of established corticosteroids showed good agreement with published PPB figures. The published PPB figure for fluticasone propionate (FP) was, however, found to be an underestimate, and this was subsequently confirmed, at clin. relevant plasma concns., to be 99.3%. The modified method was particularly suited to lead optimization because it provided samples in a consistent matrix compatible with std. high throughput LC/MS/MS anal.

      Prediction of human clearance of twenty-nine drugs from hepatic microsomal intrinsic clearance data: an examination of in vitro half-life approach and nonspecific binding to microsomes
      Obach, R. Scott
      Drug Metabolism and Disposition (1999), 27 (11), 1350-1359CODEN: DMDSAI; ISSN:0090-9556. (American Society for Pharmacology and Experimental Therapeutics)
      Twenty-nine drugs of disparate structures and physicochem. properties were used in an examn. of the capability of human liver microsomal lability data ("in vitro T1/2" approach) to be useful in the prediction of human clearance. Addnl., the potential importance of nonspecific binding to microsomes in the in vitro incubation milieu for the accurate prediction of human clearance was investigated. The compds. examd. demonstrated a wide range of microsomal metabolic labilities with scaled intrinsic clearance values ranging from less than 0.5 mL/min/kg to 189 mL/min/kg. Microsomal binding was detd. at microsomal protein concns. used in the lability incubations. For the 29 compds. studied, unbound fractions in microsomes ranged from 0.11 to 1.0. Generally, basic compds. demonstrated the greatest extent of binding and neutral and acidic compds. the least extent of binding. In the projection of human clearance values, basic and neutral compds. were well predicted when all binding considerations (blood and microsome) were disregarded, however, including both binding considerations also yielded reasonable predictions. Including only blood binding yielded very poor projections of human clearance for these two types of compds. However, for acidic compds., disregarding all binding considerations yielded poor predictions of human clearance. It was generally most difficult to accurately predict clearance for this class of compds.; however the accuracy was best when all binding considerations were included. Overall, inclusion of both blood and microsome binding values gave the best agreement between in vivo clearance values and clearance values projected from in vitro intrinsic clearance data.

        The pathology and treatment of cardiac arrhythmias: focus on atrial fibrillation
        Schmidt Constanze; Kisselbach Jana; Schweizer Patrick A; Katus Hugo A; Thomas Dierk
        Vascular health and risk management (2011), 7 (), 193-202 ISSN:.
        Atrial fibrillation (AF) is the most frequently encountered sustained cardiac arrhythmia in clinical practice and a major cause of morbidity and mortality. Effective treatment of AF still remains an unmet medical need. Treatment of AF is based on drug therapy and ablative strategies. Antiarrhythmic drug therapy is limited by a relatively high recurrence rate and proarrhythmic side effects. Catheter ablation suppresses paroxysmal AF in the majority of patients without structural heart disease but is more difficult to achieve in patients with persistent AF or with concomitant cardiac disease. Stroke is a potentially devastating complication of AF, requiring anticoagulation that harbors the risk of bleeding. In search of novel treatment modalities, targeted pharmacological treatment and gene therapy offer the potential for greater selectivity than conventional small-molecule or interventional approaches. This paper summarizes the current understanding of molecular mechanisms underlying AF. Established drug therapy and interventional treatment of AF is reviewed, and emerging clinical and experimental therapeutic approaches are highlighted.

        5
        Adverse effects of class I antiarrhythmic drugs
        Caron, Jacques; Libersa, Christian
        Drug Safety (1997), 17 (1), 8-36CODEN: DRSAEA; ISSN:0114-5916. (Adis)
        A review with 366 refs. Class I antiarrhythmic drugs are characterized by their ability to block the fast inward sodium current in cardiac muscle tissue. However, at the same time, they can be responsible for various effects involving other organs and systems. Although some of these effects can be helpful in specific situations, most of them, such as their pro-arrhythmic propensity, are deleterious. Some of the adverse effects of class I antiarrhythmic drugs are directly linked to sodium-channel blockade (conduction disorders, hemodynamic perturbations, and digestive and neurol. effects), while others are linked to other specific pharmacol. properties (e.g. atropinic, or Î±- or Î²-adrenergic blockade) or to nonspecific properties (idiosyncratic hypersensitivity, and hematol., dermatol. or hepatic reactions). Other adverse effects are assocd. with complex interactions between class I antiarrhythmics and individual predisposing factors, trigger mechanisms and physiol. factors (including concomitant drug treatment). These numerous variations and interactions within a specific environment and underlying disorder might be of pharmacol. or/and pharmacokinetic origin, making anal. of the true liability of the class I drugs very difficult when adverse effects occur.

        n-3 fatty acids, ventricular arrhythmia-related events, and fatal myocardial infarction in postmyocardial infarction patients with diabetes
        Kromhout, Daan; Geleijnse, Johanna M.; de Goede, Janette; Oude Griep, Linda M.; Mulder, Barbara J. M.; de Boer, Menko-Jan; Deckers, Jaap W.; Boersma, Eric; Zock, Peter L.; Giltay, Erik J.
        Diabetes Care (2011), 34 (12), 2515-2520CODEN: DICAD2; ISSN:0149-5992. (American Diabetes Association, Inc.)
        OBJECTIVE-We carried out a secondary anal. in high-risk patients with a previous myocardial infarction (Ml) and diabetes in the Alpha Omega Trial. We tested the hypothesis that in these patients an increased intake of the n-3 fatty acids eicosapentaenoic acid (EPA), docosahexaenoic acid (DHA), and Î±-linolenic acid (ALA) will reduce the incidence of ventricular arrhythmias and fatal MI.         Prevention of fatal cardiac arrhythmias by polyunsaturated fatty acids
        Kang, Jing X.; Leaf, Alexander
        American Journal of Clinical Nutrition (2000), 71 (1, Suppl.), 202S-207SCODEN: AJCNAC; ISSN:0002-9165. (American Society for Clinical Nutrition)
        A review with 33 refs. In animal studies, and probably in humans, dietary n-3 polyunsatd. fatty acids (PUFA) can prevent fatal ischemia-induced cardiac arrhythmias. The n-3 PUFA can also prevent heart arrhythmias in surgically prepd. conscious exercising dogs. The mechanism of the antiarrhythmic action of n-3 PUFA has been studied by the authors in spontaneously contracting cultured cardiac myocytes of neonatal rats. Adding arrhythmogenic toxins (ouabain, high Ca2+, lysophosphatidylcholine, Î²-adrenergic agonist, acylcarnitine, Ca ionophore) to the myocyte perfusate caused tachycardia, contracture, and fibrillation of the cultured myocytes. Adding eicosapentaenoic acid (EPA; 5-15 Î¼mol/L) to the superfusate before adding the toxins prevented the expected tachyarrhythmias. When the arrhythmias were first induced, adding EPA to the superfusate terminated the arrhythmias. This antiarrhythmic action occurred with dietary n-3 and n-6 PUFA; satd. fatty acids and the monounsatd. oleic acid had no such activity. Arachidonic acid (AA; C20:4n-6) effects were anomalous because in 1/3 of the tests it provoked severe arrhythmias due to cyclooxygenase metabolites of AA. When cyclooxygenase inhibitors were added with AA, the antiarrhythmic effect was like those of EPA and DHA. The action of the n-3 and n-6 PUFA is to stabilize elec. every myocyte in the heart by increasing the elec. stimulus required to elicit an action potential by â¼50% and prolonging the relative refractory time by â¼150%. These electrophysiol. effects result from an action of free PUFA to modulate sodium and calcium currents in the myocytes. PUFA also modulate sodium and calcium channels and have anticonvulsant activity in brain cells.

        Upstream Treatment of Atrial Fibrillation with n-3 Polyunsaturated Fatty Acids: Myth or Reality?
        Orso Francesco; Fabbri Gianna; Maggioni Aldo Pietro
        Arrhythmia & electrophysiology review (2015), 4 (3), 163-8 ISSN:2050-3369.
        Atrial fibrillation (AF) is the most common sustained arrhythmia in adults and is associated with an increased risk of fatal and non-fatal events. Antiarrhythmic drugs provide limited protection against AF recurrence and have a poor safety profile. Several mechanisms have been proven to be involved in AF, e.g. inflammation, oxidative stress, fibrosis and ischaemia. Prevention of AF with interventions that target these mechanisms has emerged as a result of experimental studies suggesting the use of upstream therapies. Long chain n-3 polyunsaturated fatty acids (n-3 PUFA) have multiple effects on cardiac electrophysiology, and epidemiological studies on fish oil suggest a possible use of n-3 PUFA in AF prevention. Several randomised clinical trials have been designed to evaluate the efficacy of n-3 PUFA in preventing AF. In this review, we report the conflicting results of these trials in two different clinical settings: recurrence in patients with history of AF and development of post-operative AF in patient undergoing cardiac surgery.

        von Schacky, C. Omega-3 Fatty Acids: Anti-Arrhythmic, Pro-Arrhythmic, or Both?. Front. Physiol. 2012, 3, 88, Â DOI: 10.3389/fphys.2012.00088
        [Crossref], [PubMed], [CAS],Â Google Scholar
        13
        Omega-3 Fatty acids: anti-arrhythmic, pro-arrhythmic, or both?
        von Schacky C
        Frontiers in physiology (2012), 3 (), 88 ISSN:.
        This review focuses on developments after 2008, when the topic was last reviewed by the author. Pertinent publications were found by medline searches and in the author's personal data base. Prevention of atrial fibrillation (AF) was investigated in a number of trials, sparked by one positive report on the effects of eicosapentaenoic acid (EPA) and docosahexaenoic acid (DHA), considerations of upstream therapy, data from electrophysiologic laboratories and animal experiments. If EPA + DHA prevent postoperative AF, the effect is probably smaller than initially expected. The same is probably true for maintenance of sinus rhythm after cardioversion and for new-onset AF. Larger trials are currently ongoing. Prevention of ventricular arrhythmias was studied in carriers of an implanted cardioverter-defibrillator, with no clear results. This might have been due to a broad definition of the primary endpoint, including any ventricular arrhythmia and any action of the device. Epidemiologic studies support the contention that high levels of EPA + DHA prevent sudden cardiac death (SCD). However, since SCD is a rare occurrence, it is difficult to conduct an adequately powered trial. In patients with congestive heart failure, EPA + DHA reduced total mortality and rehospitalizations, but not SCD or presumed arrhythmic death. Of three trials in patients after a myocardial infarction, two were inadequately powered, and in one, the dose might have been too low. Taken together, while epidemiologic studies support an inverse relation between EPA + DHA and occurrence of SCD or arrhythmic death, demonstrating this effect in intervention trials remained elusive so far. A pro-arrhythmic effect of EPA + DHA has not been seen in intervention studies, and results of epidemiologic and animal studies also rather argue against such an effect. A different, and probably more productive, perspective is provided by a standardized analytical assessment of a person's status in EPA + DHA by use of the omega-3 index, EPA + DHA in red cell fatty acids. In populations with a high omega-3 index, SCD is rare. Intervention trials can become more effective by including a low omega-3 index into the inclusion criteria, thus creating a study population more likely to demonstrate an effect of EPA + DHA. This is especially relevant in case of rare endpoints, like new-onset AF or SCD.

        CYP-eicosanoids-A new link between omega-3 fatty acids and cardiac disease?
        Westphal, Christina; Konkel, Anne; Schunck, Wolf-Hagen
        Prostaglandins & Other Lipid Mediators (2011), 96 (1-4), 99-108CODEN: POLMFL; ISSN:1098-8823. (Elsevier)
        A review. Fish oil omega-3 fatty acids such as eicosapentaenoic acid (EPA) and docosahexaenoic acid (DHA) protect against arrhythmia and sudden cardiac death by largely unknown mechanisms. Recent in vitro and in vivo studies demonstrate that arachidonic acid (AA) metabolizing cytochrome P 450-(CYP) enzymes accept EPA and DHA as efficient alternative substrates. Dietary EPA/DHA supplementation causes a profound shift of the cardiac CYP-eicosanoid profile from AA- to EPA- and DHA-derived epoxy- and hydroxy-metabolites. CYP2J2 and other CYP epoxygenases preferentially epoxidize the Ï-3 double bond of EPA and DHA. The corresponding metabolites, 17,18-epoxy-EPA and 19,20-epoxy-DHA, dominate the CYP-eicosanoid profile of the rat heart after EPA/DHA supplementation. The (Ï-3)-epoxyeicosanoids show highly potent antiarrhythmic properties in neonatal cardiomyocytes, suggesting that these metabolites may specifically contribute to the cardioprotective effects of omega-3 fatty acids. This hypothesis is discussed in the context of recent findings that revealed CYP-eicosanoid mediated mechanisms in cardiac ischemia-reperfusion injury and maladaptive cardiac hypertrophy.
